<div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453855 id=\"110453855\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">I present a timely spectroscopic follow-up of the newly discovered, quadruply lensed quasar PSOJ0147 from the Pan-STARRS 1 survey. The newly acquired optical spectra with GMOS onboard the Gemini North Telescope allow us to pin down the redshifts of both the foreground lensing galaxy and the background lensed quasar to be <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.572 and 2.341, providing a firm basis for cosmography with future high-cadence photometric monitoring. I also inspect difference spectra from two of the quasar images, revealing the microlensing effect. Long-term spectroscopic follow-ups will shed lights on the structure of the active galactic nucleus and its environment.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;quasars: general&quot;\">quasars: general</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmology: observation&quot;\">cosmology: observation</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453857 id=\"110453857\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In the context of precision cosmology, determining the local value of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to a percent level and comparing it with cosmic microwave background (CMB) results provides a tremendous leverage on the dark energy, neutrino physics, and the geometry of the Universe. Taking advantage of the distance ladder, the SH0ES team obtained a local measurement of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to 2.4\u2009per\u2009cent (Riess et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) is in tension (at the 3.4\u03c3 level) with the <em>Planck</em> CMB results. Before claiming that such tension is due to systematics in the <em>Planck</em> measurement, or hints to new physics, it is important to have independent local <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> measurements with a comparable precision.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this regard, the gravitational lensing time-delay method is an ideal alternative. This is because in strong lensing, lights from a multiple-image source will go through different paths (geodesics). Since light traveltimes (or time-delays) differ proportionally to the space\u2013time curvature, we can thus estimate the <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. Refsdal (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1964</a>) originally suggested to use standard candles such as supernovae to measure the time-delay. Supernovae, especially of Type Ia (SNe Ia), have several advantages in time-delay measurements (see e.g. Lee <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). First of all, SNe Ia have a distinct light-curve shape, so we can measure the time-delay accurately if they are discovered and followed up well before the light maximum. Secondly, from the empirical light curve template, we know the intrinsic brightness of the SNe, which provides the <em>magnification factor</em> that breaks the mass\u2013sheet degeneracy and yields a direct measurement of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> (Oguri &amp; Kawano <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, due to the lack of all-sky transient surveys, the first multiply lensed SN Ia, iPTF16geu, was reported only very recently (Goobar et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). The discovery of iPTF16geu demonstrated that it is very difficult to establish the lensing nature before the SN light maximum, making it difficult to measure time-delays. To make it worse, lensed SNe Ia will also suffer from microlensing effects (More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), further accurate determination of the time delay. In this regard, instead of SNe Ia, time-delay measurements were carried out with quasars as a background source. For example, the most recent time-delay measurements from the H0LiCOW team (Suyu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) demonstrated that it is possible to measure time-delays with three multiply lensed quasars, enabling an <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> estimation at 3.8\u2009per\u2009cent level (Bonvin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). This was possible because they also constrained the mass\u2013sheet effect with spectroscopic observations (Rusu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), thus were able to break the constraint of the effect of external masses along the line of sight.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the current time-delay method provides a consistent <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> estimate like the distance ladder method, we still need to obtain <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to the 1\u2009per\u2009cent level, both to understand the tension with the CMB results from the <em>Planck</em> satellite and to shed light on the dark energy. However, at present there are only a handful of suitable multiply lensed systems; hence, we need to increase the sample of suitable lensed quasars. Recently, Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) have identified a new quadruply lensed quasar candidate from the Pan-STARRS archive data. This system is of particular interests because the four lensed quasar images are bright and provide a potentially suitable case for accurate time-delay measurements. This is especially the case when we consider to adopt the new high-cadence monitoring method proposed by Courbin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this work, I present timely follow-up spectra of this new candidate, with the aim to provide accurate redshifts of both the foreground lensing galaxy and the background quasar for cosmography. This paper is organized as follows: The observation is documented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>. The analysis and results are presented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>. I give the conclusion in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453862 id=\"110453862\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 OBSERVATION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">PSOJ0147 was first reported by Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), but was invisible for an immediate follow-up at that time, and Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) derived only the redshifts of the foreground lens and the background quasar photometrically.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the lensed images are rather bright, with <em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a015.40\u201317.74\u2009mag, the foreground lens is rather faint, with <em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a019.5\u2009mag. Though Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) carried out spectroscopic observations of the lensed quasars with the Keck Cosmic Web Imager (KWCI) onboard the Keck II telescope, they only obtained a total integration time of \u223c1200\u2009s, not deep enough to detect the foreground lensing galaxy. Furthermore, Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) covered only the blue side of the optical spectrum; hence, their redshift estimate of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377 is heavily influenced by the broad absorption line (BAL) feature. Indeed, a broader wavelength coverage from the 2.5-m Nordic Optical Telescope (Lee <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) provides a more accurate redshift estimate using the cleaner forbidden line [C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], resulting in a smaller quasar redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.001. However, neither Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) nor Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) was able to detect the foreground lens spectroscopically. In this regard, I thus made use of the fast turnaround programme of the 8-m Gemini North Telescope at Maunakea, with its Gemini Multi Object Spectrograph (GMOS-N). To reveal the spectral feature of the faint foreground lensing galaxy, I obtained 4\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01200-s exposures with a 0.5-arcsec slit to cover the foreground lensing galaxy, as well as the brightest and faintest quasar images (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). The observations were carried out with the B600 grating to cover a wide wavelength range in the optical. Given the photometric redshift of the foreground lens at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.57, I expected to cover the Ca H&amp;K lines, as well as the <em>G</em>-band absorption features. In addition, with the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341, I expected to cover the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb 1908.734 \u00c5 forbidden line, to verify the redshift of the quasar, as reported by Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"110453865\" scrollto-destination=\"110453865\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=xYgs1mNi1xPx3qoMt0LEapRP3Bz8Nhu5YJZbvYv6ez~of~J2pkRYaX23XQpbGn9wFtqkBqu8Yt~JXK~viJxWpcyjic2RlP0JLfQu7UOTdVAWmrybkImLJc2JxAz5nsxgZIpLq39fqm~aHZY~Bh9U~IdSJ7zIAUc4G~098an~SPSajibU0rpqzAYtiCFcEFjr7OQO56QsmSFDJ-iKor-s0XPKGf8UboVBf7TlWbl4Tl3QWN7VpW~TcXHRxTdcv6QMJ6s1E73DWQ2F0mbTlT5RfUFLL0FTLoymsSR5COYCSoC2U1H6DRd7mbfMclVsAeDJVJXHutZe-3ViTC73DQ1hwA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Illustration of the GMOS-N spectroscopic follow-up. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The lensed quasar images are marked in A\u2013D, starting with the brightest image A (<em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a015.40\u2009mag). The 0.5-arcsecslit is shown in a violet rectangle. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The background image is a 10-s acquisition exposure in the <em>i</em> band from GMOS-N, showing a region of 10 \u00d7 10\u2009arcsec<sup>2</sup>, where north is up and east is to the left.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=xYgs1mNi1xPx3qoMt0LEapRP3Bz8Nhu5YJZbvYv6ez~of~J2pkRYaX23XQpbGn9wFtqkBqu8Yt~JXK~viJxWpcyjic2RlP0JLfQu7UOTdVAWmrybkImLJc2JxAz5nsxgZIpLq39fqm~aHZY~Bh9U~IdSJ7zIAUc4G~098an~SPSajibU0rpqzAYtiCFcEFjr7OQO56QsmSFDJ-iKor-s0XPKGf8UboVBf7TlWbl4Tl3QWN7VpW~TcXHRxTdcv6QMJ6s1E73DWQ2F0mbTlT5RfUFLL0FTLoymsSR5COYCSoC2U1H6DRd7mbfMclVsAeDJVJXHutZe-3ViTC73DQ1hwA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Illustration of the GMOS-N spectroscopic follow-up. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The background image is a 10-s acquisition exposure in the i band from GMOS-N, showing a region of 10 \u00d7 10\u2009arcsec2, where north is up and east is to the left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig1.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/110453865/sty078fig1.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig1.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"110453865\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/sty078fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&Signature=suYjauyiA23Ig3G9Ab2QxCji3fvCIFlgVPhshjJVarjpMLTYbpdSNPURvZcAejLe9Bgm-H7hpjptXnFtrU6ikgxMcnA1uLzNeewJCyMNNwG16xR4OtE5~yWB8QHJ1cXgSwrl4sPZc5hGXg6s9Hu02Va8vtZdaJJZlveKs2210Y-9c-3JDAOEHxibsmyrBE0VAdvLnVR~Q22HhW9N6Ud0X1CV74x-~t0H6GJOO6FGdUU9CzEDXD7nLm2U1Zps6P1MOcDPhErEqOsGL4d-uJCDXmMpsij72v65jn9GAM3sTjY6yCg3HKVIq9QA6SrqTYfqJcgE0MsSGIaYgGKSLL2bDA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=110453865&ar=4797181&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig1.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Illustration of the GMOS-N spectroscopic follow-up. The lensed quasar images are marked in A\u2013D, starting with the brightest image A (<em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a015.40\u2009mag). The 0.5-arcsecslit is shown in a violet rectangle. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The background image is a 10-s acquisition exposure in the <em>i</em> band from GMOS-N, showing a region of 10 \u00d7 10\u2009arcsec<sup>2</sup>, where north is up and east is to the left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The observations were carried out on 2017 September 2 with GMOS-N mounted on the Gemini North Telescope (programme ID: GN-2017B-FT-4). To avoid bad pixels and remove cosmic rays, the observations were performed using an A\u2013B\u2013B\u2013A sequence, with a 1\u2009arcsec dithering. We took 1200-s exposures at each dithering position, resulting in a total integration time of 80\u2009min.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Data reductions were carried out in a standard fashion using <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span><sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">1</a></sup> and the Gemini <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span> package,<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">2</a></sup> including subtraction of bias, flat-fielding, calibrating the wavelength using a Thorium\u2013Argon lamp, and calibrating the flux using a spectroscopic standard star EG 131.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453868 id=\"110453868\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">After data reduction, I can see traces of absorption lines from the foreground lensing galaxy, e.g. Ca H&amp;K (\u03bb\u03bb3934, 3969) and <em>G</em> band (\u03bb\u03bb4304). The reduced lens spectrum, as well as these absorption features, is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. From these absorption features, I am able to obtain the lens redshift; I use the <em>splot</em> task in <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span>, and perform a Gaussian fitting to the Ca H&amp;K and <em>G</em>-band features. After measuring the Gaussian centroids from each image, I use their mean to determine the redshift, and the standard deviation as the redshift uncertainty, resulting in a redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.5716\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0004. The values of the Gaussian centroid from each of the absorption features are shown in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"110453870\" scrollto-destination=\"110453870\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=r4oJq~35I4qTbG06BIInN7M2ABDaAG6KLD7jA8Jp69ifJXErZ~vDgitPrwdRoZLHQ6WCXwnbKZelYEgXVSoXrXSVDtD6PYDOlHoPgDIfs9Ttdw~DwkmWkgEi-MqN5Z1p-NIgsZHdUdlf5re1hKMt6xAmBg8l-iN6T91GZJZzxXgSrlhmZfsKmafBLnyi666kqIUVOfTzjWLKEEp4LGDGF-128mQQMt-7GNx3mdRRjAl5FZgcPtQkPbi1XWKekc2vY2VIre8ZSJfDKzx7IwFUGz1~dlx3I9JuwpDMWvW9wo-f7w6eIoEx1Md0z5iN4SJclImd9-T-xCnQQolXW6vhUg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy from GMOS-N onboard the Gemini North Telescope. class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/110453871\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In addition to the lens, I also obtained spectra of the brightest and the faintest quasar images (A and D). After reduction, I clearly see the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] forbidden emission line, as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. As [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] is free from BALs, it is ideal to be used to estimate redshifts. Indeed, Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) reported a quasar redshift of 2.341\u00b10.001, contrasting to earlier results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). As discussed in Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), the differences may originate from the shorter wavelength coverage, as well as contaminations from BALs in Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Here, with the new Gemini/GMOS-N spectra, I again confirm [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] of the background quasar peaks at \u223c6380\u2009\u00c5, suggesting that the background quasar is at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341, in agreement with the results from Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and smaller than the results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"110453873\" scrollto-destination=\"110453873\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=L8x~Hh7Gwt8RdbhaUgB7LpBfKvMAFnyQ-zI6zgRdYDDcZ7DljCXeKRaE21wKvtQhGS~SJ7eQFwJ-S5rRAzi51MJSkcmh~sfbZqolrwtwu9KEloZIwGLgCdtzSZDOtF~HkOXUNRL~-Swf9~-1mMqOzPOdohFZMwaBqRxsggZCU-82EbeMlVGT1UtolzKhzemma9KSrOmmGli49GvRNv9ExtLqNtKwoxVy9xgSVqW6iwrrx4VGWRtXrwjehhgsWdBN-68IEoSfZ2QJFhf~4~X--EfcpZJP249MzIrfQPWbmWf03bcyZIbLhLfyiu7eIcVa-ibAslK2e9O5xM13wyuNqQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at z\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at z\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009iii] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009iii] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=L8x~Hh7Gwt8RdbhaUgB7LpBfKvMAFnyQ-zI6zgRdYDDcZ7DljCXeKRaE21wKvtQhGS~SJ7eQFwJ-S5rRAzi51MJSkcmh~sfbZqolrwtwu9KEloZIwGLgCdtzSZDOtF~HkOXUNRL~-Swf9~-1mMqOzPOdohFZMwaBqRxsggZCU-82EbeMlVGT1UtolzKhzemma9KSrOmmGli49GvRNv9ExtLqNtKwoxVy9xgSVqW6iwrrx4VGWRtXrwjehhgsWdBN-68IEoSfZ2QJFhf~4~X--EfcpZJP249MzIrfQPWbmWf03bcyZIbLhLfyiu7eIcVa-ibAslK2e9O5xM13wyuNqQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at z\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at z\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009iii] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009iii] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009iii] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/110453873/sty078fig3.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"110453873\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&Signature=FRZz0-VIxr23AUFZIp4JkYIFdO-h4uOY5FZK1UhW1bqp7VPIyQJzOAoCqB00CiisICGM8nb~R6Xy54PKufSWLV4q~H-WbSot2keVSPGeAzu8nnKc8sDWthCfDPEmrfEdOSRLYACVNQw8vhmSaGtEcHjTDBCyFpJtaS37HSGfqaVu4-kbuI1VdG8ra9pFSIPNsjm0GygsmUNjCbXVuGiv~n875-FqAwvpe5cGYOEqHitaMQRNiRdUPJ0Uovhy0ZWutth2ihb2scAeNF8jOOCbXQr-68Gwqtdokgs6o951TpwbNEA~~AED~XSpmmEbuRNfVhahYRtzeW-W9rohoi9fpA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=110453873&ar=4797181&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">With the spectra from quasar images A and D at hand, I can also investigate the microlensing effect spectroscopically using difference spectrum. This has been done; for example, Wisotzki, Koehler &amp; Kayser (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) and Lidman et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>) have investigated the difference spectra of the doubly lensed system HE 1104\u00a0\u2212\u00a01805, and revealed the microlensing effects from spectra. Furthermore, Sluse et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) presented a spectroscopic microlensing for 17 lensed quasars. If microlensing affects the spectra, there will exist a factor <em>K</em> where spectrum A \u2212 <em>K</em> times spectrum D will cancel out the continuum and I can see only the emission line. In addition, there will also exist another <em>K</em> value where the emission lines will be cancelled out and leave only a blue residual continuum since microlensing affects most the central parts of the accretion disc. By experimenting different values of <em>K</em>, with <em>K</em> = 8, I am able to null the continuum and leave only the emission features. With <em>K</em> = 16, I can cancel out the emission feature, leaving a blue residual continuum. The difference spectra are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. These suggest that we can see traces of the microlensing effects in PSOJ0147. The continuum is more susceptible to microlensing effects. Taking the flux ratio between the continuum of images A and D, I obtain a magnitude difference of 2.26\u2009mag, close to the value of the observed quasar image magnitudes relative to image A, in particular the <em>r</em> band around 2.3\u2009mag for image D versus image A, as shown in fig. 7 of Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). On the other hand, the emission-line flux ratio of images A and D suggests a magnitude difference of 3.01\u2009mag, in the same direction (larger magnitude difference) as predicted by the lens modelling of Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453875 id=\"110453875\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">PSOJ0147 is the first quadruply lensed quasar candidate identified in the Pan-STARRS archive data. In this paper, I present follow-up spectra of PSOJ0147 using GMOS-N onboard the Gemini North Telescope. The results can be summarized as follows: <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I provided the first spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy.</em> As the foreground lensing galaxy is very faint (<em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a019.5\u2009mag), a previous spectroscopic follow-up by Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) could not reveal the spectral signature of the foreground lens. With the power of the 8-m Gemini telescopes, and with a total integration time of 80\u2009min, I was able to obtain a spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy for the first time. Using Ca H&amp;K and <em>G</em> band, I was able to determine the lens redshift to be <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.5716, close to the photo-<em>z</em> prediction by Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I confirmed the background quasar at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341.</em> For cosmographical studies, it is crucial to obtain accurate spectroscopic redshifts of both the foreground lens and the background quasar. Contrasting with the results of Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), a previous study by Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) reported a larger redshift of the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377, which is likely to be affected by the BAL nature of the background quasar, and the narrow wavelength coverage (up to C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> at the observer's frame) in Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Here, with the prominent [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission-line peaks at 6380\u2009\u00c5, I confirmed the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I demonstrated the microlensing effect in difference spectra.</em> As microlensing affects most of the central parts of the accretion disc, the continuum and the emission component of the background quasar will experience different lensing factors. I investigated the difference spectra between quasar images A and D, and showed that with A \u2212 8\u00d7D we can null the continuum, whereas with A\u00a0\u2212\u00a016\u00d7D we can cancel out the emission-line feature. Further epochs of a spectroscopic monitoring will be crucial to study the environment around the active galactic nucleus of the background quasar.</p></li></ul></p>                    <h2 <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453855 id=\"110453855\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">I present a timely spectroscopic follow-up of the newly discovered, quadruply lensed quasar PSOJ0147 from the Pan-STARRS 1 survey. The newly acquired optical spectra with GMOS onboard the Gemini North Telescope allow us to pin down the redshifts of both the foreground lensing galaxy and the background lensed quasar to be <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.572 and 2.341, providing a firm basis for cosmography with future high-cadence photometric monitoring. I also inspect difference spectra from two of the quasar images, revealing the microlensing effect. Long-term spectroscopic follow-ups will shed lights on the structure of the active galactic nucleus and its environment.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;quasars: general&quot;\">quasars: general</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmology: observation&quot;\">cosmology: observation</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453857 id=\"110453857\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In the context of precision cosmology, determining the local value of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to a percent level and comparing it with cosmic microwave background (CMB) results provides a tremendous leverage on the dark energy, neutrino physics, and the geometry of the Universe. Taking advantage of the distance ladder, the SH0ES team obtained a local measurement of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to 2.4\u2009per\u2009cent (Riess et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) is in tension (at the 3.4\u03c3 level) with the <em>Planck</em> CMB results. Before claiming that such tension is due to systematics in the <em>Planck</em> measurement, or hints to new physics, it is important to have independent local <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> measurements with a comparable precision.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this regard, the gravitational lensing time-delay method is an ideal alternative. This is because in strong lensing, lights from a multiple-image source will go through different paths (geodesics). Since light traveltimes (or time-delays) differ proportionally to the space\u2013time curvature, we can thus estimate the <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. Refsdal (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1964</a>) originally suggested to use standard candles such as supernovae to measure the time-delay. Supernovae, especially of Type Ia (SNe Ia), have several advantages in time-delay measurements (see e.g. Lee <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). First of all, SNe Ia have a distinct light-curve shape, so we can measure the time-delay accurately if they are discovered and followed up well before the light maximum. Secondly, from the empirical light curve template, we know the intrinsic brightness of the SNe, which provides the <em>magnification factor</em> that breaks the mass\u2013sheet degeneracy and yields a direct measurement of <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> (Oguri &amp; Kawano <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, due to the lack of all-sky transient surveys, the first multiply lensed SN Ia, iPTF16geu, was reported only very recently (Goobar et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). The discovery of iPTF16geu demonstrated that it is very difficult to establish the lensing nature before the SN light maximum, making it difficult to measure time-delays. To make it worse, lensed SNe Ia will also suffer from microlensing effects (More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), further accurate determination of the time delay. In this regard, instead of SNe Ia, time-delay measurements were carried out with quasars as a background source. For example, the most recent time-delay measurements from the H0LiCOW team (Suyu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) demonstrated that it is possible to measure time-delays with three multiply lensed quasars, enabling an <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> estimation at 3.8\u2009per\u2009cent level (Bonvin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). This was possible because they also constrained the mass\u2013sheet effect with spectroscopic observations (Rusu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), thus were able to break the constraint of the effect of external masses along the line of sight.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the current time-delay method provides a consistent <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> estimate like the distance ladder method, we still need to obtain <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> to the 1\u2009per\u2009cent level, both to understand the tension with the CMB results from the <em>Planck</em> satellite and to shed light on the dark energy. However, at present there are only a handful of suitable multiply lensed systems; hence, we need to increase the sample of suitable lensed quasars. Recently, Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) have identified a new quadruply lensed quasar candidate from the Pan-STARRS archive data. This system is of particular interests because the four lensed quasar images are bright and provide a potentially suitable case for accurate time-delay measurements. This is especially the case when we consider to adopt the new high-cadence monitoring method proposed by Courbin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this work, I present timely follow-up spectra of this new candidate, with the aim to provide accurate redshifts of both the foreground lensing galaxy and the background quasar for cosmography. This paper is organized as follows: The observation is documented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>. The analysis and results are presented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>. I give the conclusion in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453862 id=\"110453862\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 OBSERVATION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">PSOJ0147 was first reported by Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), but was invisible for an immediate follow-up at that time, and Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) derived only the redshifts of the foreground lens and the background quasar photometrically.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the lensed images are rather bright, with <em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a015.40\u201317.74\u2009mag, the foreground lens is rather faint, with <em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a019.5\u2009mag. Though Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) carried out spectroscopic observations of the lensed quasars with the Keck Cosmic Web Imager (KWCI) onboard the Keck II telescope, they only obtained a total integration time of \u223c1200\u2009s, not deep enough to detect the foreground lensing galaxy. Furthermore, Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) covered only the blue side of the optical spectrum; hence, their redshift estimate of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377 is heavily influenced by the broad absorption line (BAL) feature. Indeed, a broader wavelength coverage from the 2.5-m Nordic Optical Telescope (Lee <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) provides a more accurate redshift estimate using the cleaner forbidden line [C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], resulting in a smaller quasar redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.001. However, neither Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) nor Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) was able to detect the foreground lens spectroscopically. In this regard, I thus made use of the fast turnaround programme of the 8-m Gemini North Telescope at Maunakea, with its Gemini Multi Object Spectrograph (GMOS-N). To reveal the spectral feature of the faint foreground lensing galaxy, I obtained 4\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01200-s exposures with a 0.5-arcsec slit to cover the foreground lensing galaxy, as well as the brightest and faintest quasar images (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). The observations were carried out with the B600 grating to cover a wide wavelength range in the optical. Given the photometric redshift of the foreground lens at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.57, I expected to cover the Ca H&amp;K lines, as well as the <em>G</em>-band absorption features. In addition, with the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341, I expected to cover the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb 1908.734 \u00c5 forbidden line, to verify the redshift of the quasar, as reported by Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"110453865\" scrollto-destination=\"110453865\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=xYgs1mNi1xPx3qoMt0LEapRP3Bz8Nhu5YJZbvYv6ez~of~J2pkRYaX23XQpbGn9wFtqkBqu8Yt~JXK~viJxWpcyjic2RlP0JLfQu7UOTdVAWmrybkImLJc2JxAz5nsxgZIpLq39fqm~aHZY~Bh9U~IdSJ7zIAUc4G~098an~SPSajibU0rpqzAYtiCFcEFjr7OQO56QsmSFDJ-iKor-s0XPKGf8UboVBf7TlWbl4Tl3QWN7VpW~TcXHRxTdcv6QMJ6s1E73DWQ2F0mbTlT5RfUFLL0FTLoymsSR5COYCSoC2U1H6DRd7mbfMclVsAeDJVJXHutZe-3ViTC73DQ1hwA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Illustration of the GMOS-N spectroscopic follow-up. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The lensed quasar images are marked in A\u2013D, starting with the brightest image A (<em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a015.40\u2009mag). The 0.5-arcsecslit is shown in a violet rectangle. With a single slit position (position angle = 7\u00b0), we can obtain spectra of the brightest and faintest quasar image as well as of the lensing galaxy at once. The background image is a 10-s acquisition exposure in the <em>i</em> band from GMOS-N, showing a region of 10 \u00d7 10\u2009arcsec<sup>2</sup>, where north is up and east is to the left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The observations were carried out on 2017 September 2 with GMOS-N mounted on the Gemini North Telescope (programme ID: GN-2017B-FT-4). To avoid bad pixels and remove cosmic rays, the observations were performed using an A\u2013B\u2013B\u2013A sequence, with a 1\u2009arcsec dithering. We took 1200-s exposures at each dithering position, resulting in a total integration time of 80\u2009min.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Data reductions were carried out in a standard fashion using <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span><sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">1</a></sup> and the Gemini <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span> package,<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">2</a></sup> including subtraction of bias, flat-fielding, calibrating the wavelength using a Thorium\u2013Argon lamp, and calibrating the flux using a spectroscopic standard star EG 131.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453868 id=\"110453868\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">After data reduction, I can see traces of absorption lines from the foreground lensing galaxy, e.g. Ca H&amp;K (\u03bb\u03bb3934, 3969) and <em>G</em> band (\u03bb\u03bb4304). The reduced lens spectrum, as well as these absorption features, is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. From these absorption features, I am able to obtain the lens redshift; I use the <em>splot</em> task in <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span>, and perform a Gaussian fitting to the Ca H&amp;K and <em>G</em>-band features. After measuring the Gaussian centroids from each image, I use their mean to determine the redshift, and the standard deviation as the redshift uncertainty, resulting in a redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.5716\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0004. The values of the Gaussian centroid from each of the absorption features are shown in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"110453870\" scrollto-destination=\"110453870\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=r4oJq~35I4qTbG06BIInN7M2ABDaAG6KLD7jA8Jp69ifJXErZ~vDgitPrwdRoZLHQ6WCXwnbKZelYEgXVSoXrXSVDtD6PYDOlHoPgDIfs9Ttdw~DwkmWkgEi-MqN5Z1p-NIgsZHdUdlf5re1hKMt6xAmBg8l-iN6T91GZJZzxXgSrlhmZfsKmafBLnyi666kqIUVOfTzjWLKEEp4LGDGF-128mQQMt-7GNx3mdRRjAl5FZgcPtQkPbi1XWKekc2vY2VIre8ZSJfDKzx7IwFUGz1~dlx3I9JuwpDMWvW9wo-f7w6eIoEx1Md0z5iN4SJclImd9-T-xCnQQolXW6vhUg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy from GMOS-N onboard the Gemini North Telescope. Note that the lens galaxy and the lensed images of the background quasar are very close; thus, the lens galaxy spectrum is blended by the light from the quasar images. Nevertheless, this does not affect the redshift determination of the lens galaxy, as we can clearly see the absorption lines from the lens galaxy\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig2.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/110453870/sty078fig2.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig2.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"110453870\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/sty078fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&Signature=unuiBrPB47wUZ2QWnCgOzQ2WyaMlaQvutaKzvpfSX3UPkdpF3RpPdEfvHzJSeQY5ftYW6pk-5R4YliiiKim7gVyOzu6WCtB254NeICkNTbN7WchBvktZ51n800L7TUJ8XHhoVlg5wnmW1lvAhpyEgqxT0UL-MxxCQbkvIhEULAvk1GYVww0os7iO8cGgYrVsxdIhC0LSvRku0oyo-Ypham8hYq9NdaPMHFLdWfG79f4wf~CILxAvqWtTsOz4H0iFhgD4l9Ock8U~CgwraIkcSezNBMCEUArvbbN2Yoq2e~TDmXB2GpJM0JCqhhT03kcBolOH8TMEiQ8DsZXZ6Q2QKQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=110453870&ar=4797181&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig2.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy from GMOS-N onboard the Gemini North Telescope. none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ca H \u03bb\u03bb3934\u00a0</td><td>6182.68\u00a0</td><td>0.5716\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>Ca K \u03bb\u03bb3969\u00a0</td><td>6236.02\u00a0</td><td>0.5712\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td><em>G-</em> band \u03bb\u03bb4304\u00a0</td><td>6766.1\u00a0</td><td>0.5720\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/110453871\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><div class=\"table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table 1.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Gaussian centroid of absorption features in the lens spectrum.</p></div> </div><div class=\"table-overflow\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr><tr><th>Lines<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03bb<sub>obs</sub> (\u00c5)<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>z</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ca H \u03bb\u03bb3934\u00a0</td><td>6182.68\u00a0</td><td>0.5716\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>Ca K \u03bb\u03bb3969\u00a0</td><td>6236.02\u00a0</td><td>0.5712\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td><em>G-</em> band \u03bb\u03bb4304\u00a0</td><td>6766.1\u00a0</td><td>0.5720\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr><tr><th>Lines<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>\u03bb<sub>obs</sub> (\u00c5)<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th><em>z</em><span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ca H \u03bb\u03bb3934\u00a0</td><td>6182.68\u00a0</td><td>0.5716\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>Ca K \u03bb\u03bb3969\u00a0</td><td>6236.02\u00a0</td><td>0.5712\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td><em>G-</em> band \u03bb\u03bb4304\u00a0</td><td>6766.1\u00a0</td><td>0.5720\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/110453871\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In addition to the lens, I also obtained spectra of the brightest and the faintest quasar images (A and D). After reduction, I clearly see the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] forbidden emission line, as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. As [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] is free from BALs, it is ideal to be used to estimate redshifts. Indeed, Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) reported a quasar redshift of 2.341\u00b10.001, contrasting to earlier results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). As discussed in Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), the differences may originate from the shorter wavelength coverage, as well as contaminations from BALs in Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Here, with the new Gemini/GMOS-N spectra, I again confirm [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] of the background quasar peaks at \u223c6380\u2009\u00c5, suggesting that the background quasar is at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341, in agreement with the results from Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and smaller than the results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"110453873\" scrollto-destination=\"110453873\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=L8x~Hh7Gwt8RdbhaUgB7LpBfKvMAFnyQ-zI6zgRdYDDcZ7DljCXeKRaE21wKvtQhGS~SJ7eQFwJ-S5rRAzi51MJSkcmh~sfbZqolrwtwu9KEloZIwGLgCdtzSZDOtF~HkOXUNRL~-Swf9~-1mMqOzPOdohFZMwaBqRxsggZCU-82EbeMlVGT1UtolzKhzemma9KSrOmmGli49GvRNv9ExtLqNtKwoxVy9xgSVqW6iwrrx4VGWRtXrwjehhgsWdBN-68IEoSfZ2QJFhf~4~X--EfcpZJP249MzIrfQPWbmWf03bcyZIbLhLfyiu7eIcVa-ibAslK2e9O5xM13wyuNqQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at z\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at z\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009iii] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009iii] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. If the quasar were at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/m_sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&amp;Signature=L8x~Hh7Gwt8RdbhaUgB7LpBfKvMAFnyQ-zI6zgRdYDDcZ7DljCXeKRaE21wKvtQhGS~SJ7eQFwJ-S5rRAzi51MJSkcmh~sfbZqolrwtwu9KEloZIwGLgCdtzSZDOtF~HkOXUNRL~-Swf9~-1mMqOzPOdohFZMwaBqRxsggZCU-82EbeMlVGT1UtolzKhzemma9KSrOmmGli49GvRNv9ExtLqNtKwoxVy9xgSVqW6iwrrx4VGWRtXrwjehhgsWdBN-68IEoSfZ2QJFhf~4~X--EfcpZJP249MzIrfQPWbmWf03bcyZIbLhLfyiu7eIcVa-ibAslK2e9O5xM13wyuNqQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at z\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at z\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009iii] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009iii] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009iii] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009iii] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009iii] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/110453873/sty078fig3.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"110453873\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/475/3/10.1093_mnras_sty078/2/sty078fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611466267&Signature=FRZz0-VIxr23AUFZIp4JkYIFdO-h4uOY5FZK1UhW1bqp7VPIyQJzOAoCqB00CiisICGM8nb~R6Xy54PKufSWLV4q~H-WbSot2keVSPGeAzu8nnKc8sDWthCfDPEmrfEdOSRLYACVNQw8vhmSaGtEcHjTDBCyFpJtaS37HSGfqaVu4-kbuI1VdG8ra9pFSIPNsjm0GygsmUNjCbXVuGiv~n875-FqAwvpe5cGYOEqHitaMQRNiRdUPJ0Uovhy0ZWutth2ihb2scAeNF8jOOCbXQr-68Gwqtdokgs6o951TpwbNEA~~AED~XSpmmEbuRNfVhahYRtzeW-W9rohoi9fpA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=110453873&ar=4797181&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty078fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">From the top to bottom: (1) spectrum of the brightest quasar image A, showing the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the black label), confirming the quasar to be at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341. If the quasar were at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377 as Rubin et\u00a0al. suggested, the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line would be at 6447\u2009\u00c5 (marked by the grey label), completely offset from the peak of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line shown in the plot; (2) spectrum of the faintest quasar image D, with the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line around 6380\u2009\u00c5; (3) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 8\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the continuum and revealing only the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line; and (4) difference spectrum of image A \u2013 16\u00d7 the flux of image D, nulling the flux contribution from the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line and revealing only the flux contribution from continuum. I can see a residual continuum bluewards of the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line, suggesting microlensing effect.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">With the spectra from quasar images A and D at hand, I can also investigate the microlensing effect spectroscopically using difference spectrum. This has been done; for example, Wisotzki, Koehler &amp; Kayser (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) and Lidman et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>) have investigated the difference spectra of the doubly lensed system HE 1104\u00a0\u2212\u00a01805, and revealed the microlensing effects from spectra. Furthermore, Sluse et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) presented a spectroscopic microlensing for 17 lensed quasars. If microlensing affects the spectra, there will exist a factor <em>K</em> where spectrum A \u2212 <em>K</em> times spectrum D will cancel out the continuum and I can see only the emission line. In addition, there will also exist another <em>K</em> value where the emission lines will be cancelled out and leave only a blue residual continuum since microlensing affects most the central parts of the accretion disc. By experimenting different values of <em>K</em>, with <em>K</em> = 8, I am able to null the continuum and leave only the emission features. With <em>K</em> = 16, I can cancel out the emission feature, leaving a blue residual continuum. The difference spectra are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. These suggest that we can see traces of the microlensing effects in PSOJ0147. The continuum is more susceptible to microlensing effects. Taking the flux ratio between the continuum of images A and D, I obtain a magnitude difference of 2.26\u2009mag, close to the value of the observed quasar image magnitudes relative to image A, in particular the <em>r</em> band around 2.3\u2009mag for image D versus image A, as shown in fig. 7 of Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). On the other hand, the emission-line flux ratio of images A and D suggests a magnitude difference of 3.01\u2009mag, in the same direction (larger magnitude difference) as predicted by the lens modelling of Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453875 id=\"110453875\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">PSOJ0147 is the first quadruply lensed quasar candidate identified in the Pan-STARRS archive data. In this paper, I present follow-up spectra of PSOJ0147 using GMOS-N onboard the Gemini North Telescope. The results can be summarized as follows: <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I provided the first spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy.</em> As the foreground lensing galaxy is very faint (<em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a019.5\u2009mag), a previous spectroscopic follow-up by Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) could not reveal the spectral signature of the foreground lens. With the power of the 8-m Gemini telescopes, and with a total integration time of 80\u2009min, I was able to obtain a spectrum of the foreground lensing galaxy for the first time. Using Ca H&amp;K and <em>G</em> band, I was able to determine the lens redshift to be <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.5716, close to the photo-<em>z</em> prediction by Berghea et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>).</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I confirmed the background quasar at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341.</em> For cosmographical studies, it is crucial to obtain accurate spectroscopic redshifts of both the foreground lens and the background quasar. Contrasting with the results of Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), a previous study by Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) reported a larger redshift of the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.377, which is likely to be affected by the BAL nature of the background quasar, and the narrow wavelength coverage (up to C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> at the observer's frame) in Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Here, with the prominent [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission-line peaks at 6380\u2009\u00c5, I confirmed the background quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>I demonstrated the microlensing effect in difference spectra.</em> As microlensing affects most of the central parts of the accretion disc, the continuum and the emission component of the background quasar will experience different lensing factors. I investigated the difference spectra between quasar images A and D, and showed that with A \u2212 8\u00d7D we can null the continuum, whereas with A\u00a0\u2212\u00a016\u00d7D we can cancel out the emission-line feature. Further epochs of a spectroscopic monitoring will be crucial to study the environment around the active galactic nucleus of the background quasar.</p></li></ul></p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=110453877 id=\"110453877\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgements</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">C-HL is indebted to the referee, whose comments greatly quasar images (A and D). After reduction, I clearly see the [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] forbidden emission line, as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. As [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] is free from BALs, it is ideal to be used to estimate redshifts. Indeed, Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) reported a quasar redshift of 2.341\u00b10.001, contrasting to earlier results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). As discussed in Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), the differences may originate from the shorter wavelength coverage, as well as contaminations from BALs in Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Here, with the new Gemini/GMOS-N spectra, I again confirm [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] of the background quasar peaks at \u223c6380\u2009\u00c5, suggesting that the background quasar is at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.341, in agreement with the results from Lee (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>) and smaller than the results from Rubin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>)."}, "Pandas format": {"Table 2": {"Status": "Complete", "Action": "Processed via pandas", "Inspection": {"Notes": {"Skip cause table not important": ""}}}}}}, "Tables status": "Complete", "Paper Overview": {"type_of_reference": "JOUR", "Title": "A closer look at the quadruply lensed quasar PSOJ0147: spectroscopic redshifts and microlensing effect", "authors": ["Lee, Chien-Hsiu"], "author_address": ["AA(Subaru Telescope, National Astronomical Observatory of Japan, 650 North A'ohoku Place, Hilo, HI 96720, USA)"], "journal_name": "Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society", "volume": "475", "publication_year": "2018/04/1", "start_page": "3086", "end_page": "3089", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "quasars: general", "cosmology: observation", "Astrophysics - Astrophysics of Galaxies"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018MNRAS.475.3086L", "abstract": "I present a timely spectroscopic follow-up of the newly discovered, quadruply lensed quasar PSOJ0147 from the Pan-STARRS 1 survey. The newly acquired optical spectra with GMOS onboard the Gemini North Telescope allow us to pin down the redshifts of both the foreground lensing galaxy and the background lensed quasar to be z = 0.572 and 2.341, providing a firm basis for cosmography with future high-cadence photometric monitoring. I also inspect difference spectra from two of the quasar images, revealing the microlensing effect.
